  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f06fe2a2-9b7e-4a3e-a1af-37dcfe732a59'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="review_2312837090" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2312837090" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2312837090?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/18275478-robert-frost-selected-poems">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=modernist-movement">modernist-movement</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2312837090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10846058357264024300">I love this book. And I love the way in which Frost writes poetry. <br/><br/>The success of the poems resides in their ability to speak to the reader; they have a certain sense of universal quality as they evoke profound mental states. He does this through using objects, often natural ones, to describe a certain human emotion or feeling. A tree in the wind embodies indecision and the inability to move on. A road split in two represents a part of the human experience that I can now only think of in the ter</span>
  <span id="freeText10846058357264024300" style="display:none">I love this book. And I love the way in which Frost writes poetry. <br><br>The success of the poems resides in their ability to speak to the reader; they have a certain sense of universal quality as they evoke profound mental states. He does this through using objects, often natural ones, to describe a certain human emotion or feeling. A tree in the wind embodies indecision and the inability to move on. A road split in two represents a part of the human experience that I can now only think of in the terms Frost put them in. <br><br>I am, of course, referring to one of his most famous poems:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1519899666i/25142360._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="275" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><i>"Two roads diverged in a yellow wood,<br>And sorry I could not travel both<br>And be one traveler, long I stood<br>And looked down one as far as I could<br>To where it bent in the undergrowth;<br><br>Then took the other, as just as fair,<br>And having perhaps the better claim,<br>Because it was grassy and wanted wear;<br>Though as for that the passing there<br>Had worn them really about the same,<br><br>And both that morning equally lay<br>In leaves no step had trodden black.<br>Oh, I kept the first for another day!<br>Yet knowing how way leads on to way,<br>I doubted if I should ever come back.<br><br>I shall be telling this with a sigh<br>Somewhere ages and ages hence:<br>Two roads diverged in a wood, and I—<br>I took the one less travelled by,<br>And that has made all the difference.”</i><br><br>I remember the first time I read those lines, I was wrought with regret: regret for a decision I had not yet made. And that bespeaks the power of these words. They are beyond time and place; they demonstrate the interconnectedness of all things: they strike directly at conformity. Memory and regret can be triggered by such a simple image, one that Frost has captured all so perfectly. <br><br>Although a modernist writer, Frost did not abandon all poetical tradition like his peers. His writing is a hybrid of new and old. The language is simple and direct, though it also carries with it the weight of the past. At times he writes in blank verse and at other times he experiments with form and style. For me this speaks of a certain sense of integrity; he was not quite ready to turn his back on generation, after generation, of poetical tradition: he used it to inform and improve his modern work. It also means that his poetry does not all feel the same; some poems are vastly different to each other. This meant that the collection stayed rather fresh even towards the end. <br><br>I read this book over a number of years. I first picked it up back in 2015 and I think my appreciation of Frost has only grown since. Whilst he is not my favourite poet, I certainly count him amongst my top five. I just love the way he uses the natural world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10846058357264024300" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_328967178" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/328967178" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/328967178?book_show_action=true">Mar 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780030120602" href="/book/show/451486.Selected_Poems_of_Robert_Frost">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=bilingual">bilingual</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=united-states">united-states</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer328967178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17088371606994009260">The Selected Poems of Robert Frost, Robert Frost<br/><br/>Robert Frost, the quintessential poet of New England, was born in San Francisco in 1874. He was educated at Dartmouth College and Harvard University. Although he managed to support himself working solely as a poet for most of his life and holding various posts with a number of universities, as a young man he was employed as a bobbin boy in a mill, a cobbler, a schoolteacher, and a farmer. Frost, whose poetry focuses on natural images of New England</span>
  <span id="freeText17088371606994009260" style="display:none">The Selected Poems of Robert Frost, Robert Frost<br /><br />Robert Frost, the quintessential poet of New England, was born in San Francisco in 1874. He was educated at Dartmouth College and Harvard University. Although he managed to support himself working solely as a poet for most of his life and holding various posts with a number of universities, as a young man he was employed as a bobbin boy in a mill, a cobbler, a schoolteacher, and a farmer. Frost, whose poetry focuses on natural images of New England, received the Pulitzer Prize for Poetry four times for: New Hampshire, Collected Poems, A Further Range, and A Witness Tree. <br /><br />His works are noted for combining characteristics of both romanticism and modernism. He also wrote A Boy's Will, North of Boston, Mountain Interval, and The Gift Outright, among others. Frost married Elinor Miriam White in 1895, and they had six children--Elliott, Lesley, Carol, Irma, Marjorie, and Elinor Bettina. He died in Boston in 1963.<br /><br /><i>"One of my wishes is that those dark trees,<br />So old and firm they scarcely show the breeze,<br />Were not, as it were, the merest mask of gloom,<br />But stretched away unto the edge of doom.<br /><br />I do not see why I should ever turn back,<br />Or those should not set forth upon my track<br />To overtake me, who should miss me here<br />And long to know if still I held them dear.<br /><br />They would not find me changed from him they knew-<br />Only more sure of all I thought was true."</i><br /><br />عنوانها: «ب‍ه‍ت‍ری‍ن‌ اش‍ع‍ار راب‍رت‌ ف‍راس‍ت»‌؛ «گ‍زی‍ن‍ه‌ اش‍ع‍ار راب‍رت‌ ف‍راس‍ت»‌؛ «راه ناپیموده»؛ تاریخ خوانش روز دوم ماه فوریه سال 2002 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: ب‍ه‍ت‍ری‍ن‌ اش‍ع‍ار راب‍رت‌ ف‍راس‍ت‌؛ ت‍رج‍م‍ه‌ ف‍ت‍ح‌ال‍ل‍ه‌ م‍ج‍ت‍ب‍ائ‍ی‌؛ زی‍رن‍ظر س‍ی‍روس‌ پ‍ره‍ام‌؛ تهران، سخن؛ 1338؛ در 212ص؛ دوزبانه، فارسی انگلیسی؛<br /><br />عنوان: گ‍زی‍ن‍ه‌ اش‍ع‍ار راب‍رت‌ ف‍راس‍ت‌؛ شاعر: رابرت فراست؛ مت‍رج‍م ف‍ت‍ح‌ال‍ل‍ه‌ م‍ج‍ت‍ب‍ائ‍ی‌؛ زی‍رن‍ظر: س‍ی‍روس‌ پ‍ره‍ام‌؛ ت‍ه‍ران‌: م‍رواری‍د، 1380؛ در 221ص، مصور؛ شابک 9646026796؛ عنوان روی جلد گزیده اشعار رابرت فراست؛ موضوع شعر شاعران ایالات متحده آمریکا - سده 20م<br /><br />عنوان: راه‌ ن‍اپ‍ی‍م‍وده‌: گ‍زی‍ده‌ ای‌ از اش‍ع‍ار راب‍رت‌ ف‍راس‍ت‌ و وی‍ل‍ی‍ام‌ وردزورث‌؛ ت‍رج‍م‍ه‌ م‍ن‍ظوم‌ ب‍ه‍ن‍ام‌ م‍ق‍دم‌ (م‌. ره‍ا)؛ تهران، جنگل، 1385؛ در 80ص؛ شابک 9648917671؛<br /><br />رابرت فراست شاعر آمریکایی است که در این کتاب برگزیده‌ ای از اشعار او به زبان اصلی همراه با ترجمه فارسی فراهم آمده است؛ کتاب دارای سی و نه قطعه شعر است که همراه با معرفی شاعر در مقدمه، منتشر شده است<br /><br />آغازین کتاب با عنوان «راهی که اختیار نکرد»؛<br /><i>The Road Not Taken</i><br />نام دارد<br /><i><br />در جنگلی زرد، دو راه از هم جدا می‌شوند، و دریغا، من نمی‌توانستم که یک مسافر باشم؛ و از هر دو راه سفر کنم؛ مدتی بر جای ایستادم و تا چشمم می‌دید، به آنجایی که؛ یکی از دو راه در میان بوته‌ زارها می‌پیچید، نگریستم؛ سپس راه دیگر را در پیش گرفتم، که به همان خوبی بود، و شاید شایستگی بیشتری هم داشت؛ زیرا گیاه بسیار بر آن روییده بود، و می‌بایست که پایکوب شود ...؛ </i><br />رابرت فراست<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 27/04/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1968027319?book_show_action=true">Sep 18, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22583912-fareya?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1968027319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3166017074208652807">It is not without excellent reason that Robert Frost is considered one of the greatest American poets of the century. <br/><br/>What impresses me most about his poems is the usage of simple and colloquial words to portray ordinary, everyday incidents that have a deeper, denser meaning hidden within. Clear and conversational lines conceal subtle philosophies on life and complex social issues at their core. Also, I find myself completely drawn to the stunning picture he paints with his expressive poems on n</span>
  <span id="freeText3166017074208652807" style="display:none">It is not without excellent reason that Robert Frost is considered one of the greatest American poets of the century. <br /><br />What impresses me most about his poems is the usage of simple and colloquial words to portray ordinary, everyday incidents that have a deeper, denser meaning hidden within. Clear and conversational lines conceal subtle philosophies on life and complex social issues at their core. Also, I find myself completely drawn to the stunning picture he paints with his expressive poems on nature and those beautiful, beautiful illustrations of the New England seasons and scenery. <br /><br /><i>"Stopping by Woods on a Snowy Evening"</i>, <i>"The Road Not Taken"</i> and <i>"Fire and Ice"</i> are some of Frost's most read and loved poems. But along with these, and many more of course, one that really stands out as a personal favorite is <i>"Reluctance"</i> <br /><br /><i>Out through the fields and the woods<br /> And over the walls I have wended;<br />I have climbed the hills of view<br /> And looked at the world, and descended;<br />I have come by the highway home,<br /> And lo, it is ended.<br /> <br />The leaves are all dead on the ground,<br /> Save those that the oak is keeping<br />To ravel them one by one<br /> And let them go scraping and creeping<br />Out over the crusted snow,<br /> When others are sleeping.<br /> <br />And the dead leaves lie huddled and still,<br /> No longer blown hither and thither;<br />The last lone aster is gone;<br /> The flowers of the witch hazel wither;<br />The heart is still aching to seek,<br /> But the feet question ‘Whither?’<br /> <br />Ah, when to the heart of man<br /> Was it ever less than a treason<br />To go with the drift of things,<br /> To yield with a grace to reason,<br />And bow and accept the end<br /> Of a love or a season?</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="3166017074208652807" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3096520383" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3590312995913639118"><br/>Love has earth to which she clings <br/>With hills and circling arms about— <br/>Wall within wall to shut fear out. <br/>But Thought has need of no such things, <br/>For Thought has a pair of dauntless wings.<br/> <br/>On snow and sand and turf, I see <br/>Where Love has left a printed trace <br/>With straining in the world’s embrace. <br/>And such is Love and glad to be. <br/>But Thought has shaken his ankles free.<br/> <br/>Thought cleaves the interstellar gloom <br/>And sits in Sirius’ disc all night, <br/>Till day makes him retrace his flight, <br/>With smell of bu</span>
  <span id="freeText3590312995913639118" style="display:none"><br />Love has earth to which she clings <br />With hills and circling arms about— <br />Wall within wall to shut fear out. <br />But Thought has need of no such things, <br />For Thought has a pair of dauntless wings.<br /> <br />On snow and sand and turf, I see <br />Where Love has left a printed trace <br />With straining in the world’s embrace. <br />And such is Love and glad to be. <br />But Thought has shaken his ankles free.<br /> <br />Thought cleaves the interstellar gloom <br />And sits in Sirius’ disc all night, <br />Till day makes him retrace his flight, <br />With smell of burning on every plume, <br />Back past the sun to an earthly room.<br /> <br />His gains in heaven are what they are. <br />Yet some say Love by being thrall <br />And simply staying possesses all <br />In several beauty that Thought fares far<br />To find fused in another star. <br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1853340055?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1853340055" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3050883917381607512">I enjoyed the themes rather than the actual poetry. Frost seems to write poems made to be dissected in English classes. Oftentimes they are set up more as short stories, heavy on the dialogue, than poems. In fact, not that I'm a poetry expert, I could not detect what exactly made some of these poems verse rather than just spaced-out prose. I had a difficult time understanding a lot of these poems, but there were some that were really lovely. I'd be interested in rereading this collection in the </span>
  <span id="freeText3050883917381607512" style="display:none">I enjoyed the themes rather than the actual poetry. Frost seems to write poems made to be dissected in English classes. Oftentimes they are set up more as short stories, heavy on the dialogue, than poems. In fact, not that I'm a poetry expert, I could not detect what exactly made some of these poems verse rather than just spaced-out prose. I had a difficult time understanding a lot of these poems, but there were some that were really lovely. I'd be interested in rereading this collection in the future, but not picking up any more of Frost's poetry.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3053322548684178818">Drama: awesome. Form: pleasurable. Theme: Got a little fed up with all that personifying nature/alackadaying that nature can't be personified. Here's the best antidote:<br/><br/>It's a strange courage<br/>you give me, ancient star:<br/><br/>Shine alone in the sunrise<br/>toward which you lend no part!<br/><b>I</b><br/>Shine alone, shine nakedly, shine like bronze<br/>that reflects neither my face nor any inner part<br/>of my being, shine like fire, that mirrors nothing.<br/><b>II</b><br/>Lend no part to any humanity that suffuses<br/>you in its own light.<br/>Be not chimera of </span>
  <span id="freeText3053322548684178818" style="display:none">Drama: awesome. Form: pleasurable. Theme: Got a little fed up with all that personifying nature/alackadaying that nature can't be personified. Here's the best antidote:<br /><br />It's a strange courage<br />you give me, ancient star:<br /><br />Shine alone in the sunrise<br />toward which you lend no part!<br /><b>I</b><br />Shine alone, shine nakedly, shine like bronze<br />that reflects neither my face nor any inner part<br />of my being, shine like fire, that mirrors nothing.<br /><b>II</b><br />Lend no part to any humanity that suffuses<br />you in its own light.<br />Be not chimera of morning,<br />Half-man, half-star.<br />Be not an intelligence,<br />Like a widow's bird<br />Or an old horse.<br /><br />WALLACE STEVENS<br /><i>Nuances of a Theme by Williams</i></span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Leather Bound 9781435158962" href="/book/show/25668510-robert-frost-selected-poems">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3520411106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17365104615531191545">A couple months ago I read a collection of E.E. Cummings poems and I mentioned how my Grammy had seemed surprised by my interest in the poet. She said, she just thought Robert Frost would be more my style (her favorite). And, now we're here.<br/><br/>This collection is small and compact and I don't know how many poems it's missing from being a complete collection, but I can tell you with almost upmost certainty I will not be exploring a full collection. <br/><br/>It feels wrong to give this book 1 star, I mean it's</span>
  <span id="freeText17365104615531191545" style="display:none">A couple months ago I read a collection of E.E. Cummings poems and I mentioned how my Grammy had seemed surprised by my interest in the poet. She said, she just thought Robert Frost would be more my style (her favorite). And, now we're here.<br /><br />This collection is small and compact and I don't know how many poems it's missing from being a complete collection, but I can tell you with almost upmost certainty I will not be exploring a full collection. <br /><br />It feels wrong to give this book 1 star, I mean it's <i>Robert Frost!</i> We learn about him in school, we're taught to think of him as something great- at least I was. But, if we're being completely honest I was just <b>so bored</b>. I most definitely preferred the shorter poems, as the longer ones would take me pages to complete and I would lose all interest in the story. Most of the time I didn't have much interest at all. <br /><br />Prior to reading this book I knew two of the poems in the this collection (The Road Less Traveled and Fire &amp; Ice). After reading through the whole collection I can say they're still my favorites. Actually Fire and Ice comes in first (I like the language. I liked the whole I'd chose plan A, but I mean plan B could also work-no commitment to one plan thing a bit less). As for the Road Less Traveled by, I like that quote, but even that poem just seemed like too many words then what we needed. 🤷🏻‍♀️<br /><br />I don't know. I'm not saying Frost isn't an important or talented poet, but he's just not my kind of poet. If I had explored his work in an academic setting, with lectures and discussion maybe my vote could be swayed, but also maybe not.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17365104615531191545" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2228118776" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10959250608519209368">Well, this is a lovely collection of poems to read slowly, savoring the ones that just hit you as "special." The book even smells good, weird as that may sound. The pages just have this perfect "book smell" that made picking up this book even better.<br/><br/>There are definitely some good poems in here, like the classic "Birches," "Mending Wall," "Design," and "After Apple-Picking", and there are some lesser-known ones I discovered I really liked as well--"Snow Dust,""Evening in a Sugar Orchard," and "Go</span>
  <span id="freeText10959250608519209368" style="display:none">Well, this is a lovely collection of poems to read slowly, savoring the ones that just hit you as "special." The book even smells good, weird as that may sound. The pages just have this perfect "book smell" that made picking up this book even better.<br /><br />There are definitely some good poems in here, like the classic "Birches," "Mending Wall," "Design," and "After Apple-Picking", and there are some lesser-known ones I discovered I really liked as well--"Snow Dust,""Evening in a Sugar Orchard," and "Going for Water."<br /><br />Plus, while some poems celebrated winter, a lot of the other poems gave me a wonderful hope for spring. :) Which is EXCELLENT because, right now, there's like a -10 degree windchill outside my house.<br /><br />However...my favorite poem of his, "Stopping by a Woods on a Snowy Evening," was not included in this early collection of his--probably because that is a later poem. Still, I miss that one. That would make this collection complete. <br /><br />Also, I don't think this can really be called an "illustrated edition," which implies that the illustrations are taken directly from the poems. Not true. While the illustrations are lovely, some are repeated, and most don't directly match the poems on the page with the pictures.<br /><br />Still a lovely, lovely copy. I'd recommend to anyone who likes poetry, especially lovers of Robert Frost or nature.</span>

(Note: Why, yes! I hate cough-syrup. I'll never forget the fateful night where I spit a mouthful of it across the bathroom. Good times...)   <span id="freeText14411628251948224873" style="display:none">Ah, Robert Frost: the man who convinced me that I hated poetry. <br />Years ago, I was assigned to read some of his poetry for school and, let's just say, I read them as if I was taking cough-syrup. (Note: Why, yes! I hate cough-syrup. I'll never forget the fateful night where I spit a mouthful of it across the bathroom. Good times...) Anyhow, so I didn't enjoy his poems because I was reading them with the wrong attitude &amp;c. If Carl Sandburg hadn't come along later and shown me what a wonderful thing poetry can be, I don't know where I'd be today!<br /><br />So, while rummaging around the attic the other month, I came across this hated volume of poetry and decided to give ol' Robert another try. Guess what? I didn't hate him! He'll never be my favourite poet, but I don't hate him. (I didn't really care for the selections from <i>North of Boston</i>, but the other ones were good! The poem "Birches" was my favourite, I think.)</span>

I’m glad to own this little collection that holds some of my favorite works of his.<br/><br/><i>The melancholy of having to count souls<br/>Where they grow fewer and fewer every year<br/>Is extreme where they shrink to none at all.<br/>It must be I want life to go on living.</i><br/>- from The Census-Taker<br/><br/>I always find myself wanting to <i>pore</i> over his poems, to dissect them to find just how much they mean to me and what I take away from them.   <span id="freeText15225106151100309309" style="display:none">Something about Frost’s poems is comfortable, like coming home. I spent some days in class doing just that - analyzing his poetry. He’s one of the greats for good reason, and I find myself loving his short prose with each new-to-me one I find.<br /><br />One thing I noticed is his ability to depict fear and anxiety. Numerous pieces in this collection reflect those emotions and it feels like an echo to how many of us feel today. It speaks to the transcendence of his work that its impact is still felt so strongly.</span>

<div id="review_3461259841" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3461259841" />
    <a title="Mariah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3534760-mariah"><img alt="Mariah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1389924396p2/3534760.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3461259841?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mariah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mariah" href="/user/show/3534760-mariah">Mariah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781454929123" href="/book/show/35566738-selected-poems-of-robert-frost">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3461259841" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16449834092850212621">So much easier to appreciate now than when I was younger. Many poems are such simple pleasures, especially when I abandon the habitual pause and cadence between the lines. Mountain, Mending Wall, Blueberries, In Hardwood Groves, October, Bond and Free, are all favorites. The Last Word of a Bluebird is a children’s poem, so it does need a soft lilt and rhythm. Meeting and Passing has such resonance for trail-walkers during this pandemic (even though we rarely encounter parasols in my community). </span>
  <span id="freeText16449834092850212621" style="display:none">So much easier to appreciate now than when I was younger. Many poems are such simple pleasures, especially when I abandon the habitual pause and cadence between the lines. Mountain, Mending Wall, Blueberries, In Hardwood Groves, October, Bond and Free, are all favorites. The Last Word of a Bluebird is a children’s poem, so it does need a soft lilt and rhythm. Meeting and Passing has such resonance for trail-walkers during this pandemic (even though we rarely encounter parasols in my community). My favorite, which elicited tears, is A Line-Storm Song, which begs for a return to frivolous youthful passion in the billows of a pending torrent.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16449834092850212621" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1627933579" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1627933579" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39346323-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513962691p2/39346323.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1627933579?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/39346323-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781566190381" href="/book/show/1703661.Selected_Poems">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39346323-greg?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1627933579" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14345213934659630793">UPDATE review :<br/>I found myself yesterday in 2 discussions here on goodreads. The first involved the importance of plot in the written word (crime fiction) and the second involved the absence of concrete actions and concrete realities in writing in general, specifically contemporary fiction novels. We all read for various reasons (some of us read cereal box contents at breakfast, for example, to avoid conversation in the rest of the room or to just read something, anything, cause we MUST read some</span>
  <span id="freeText14345213934659630793" style="display:none">UPDATE review :<br />I found myself yesterday in 2 discussions here on goodreads. The first involved the importance of plot in the written word (crime fiction) and the second involved the absence of concrete actions and concrete realities in writing in general, specifically contemporary fiction novels. We all read for various reasons (some of us read cereal box contents at breakfast, for example, to avoid conversation in the rest of the room or to just read something, anything, cause we MUST read something, anything, all the time). I could think of no good examples of great books without plots or great fiction novels (imo, that is), but this volume by Frost has remained in my nightstand for many, many years. Most books of poetry have no specific plot (although they may have a central theme, like nature) and we don't read poetry (in general) looking for a plot but instead ideas, of ways to see things from a new perspective. For me, I must have a plot in crime novels, but that's just one of five factors I look for in crime novels, the other four being hook, pace, characters, and atmosphere (physical or mental). All five must be present in a crime novel for me: the best ones hook me on page one, refuse to let me go until I've finished in one sitting (with bathroom breaks for snacks), have a plot that I enjoy resolving and that the author offers me facts along the way (along with red herrings), have characters that resonate for months (over even years, such as Lizbeth in "The Girl" series), and have atmospheres (creepy scenes or extremely intense thrillers) that become part of my world. Poetry is essentially a genre presenting ideas, visions in new tones from different perspectives. Occasionally novels with little to no action come along that are loaded with ideas and suggestions about things that might have happened, forcing us to speculate on various ideas (like Murakami's "Killing Commendatore). Some argue that since nothing is real, that action scenes don't follow action scenes, they didn't like Murakami's work. I found it full of ideas, and it borders on poetry, painting visions of things that might have happen, that might will happen. I got up this morning to just shut down my access to goodreads. I've been asked to end buddy reads in the past, I've been thrown out of group reads, I get very few likes, and I generate extremely polar responses. But for these reasons, I'm glad I'm hear. People say they are insulted because I felt like a book read at a low Kincaid level. (The average best-seller today reads at a 7th great level, according to Kincaid research, opposed to 50 years ago, when the average best-seller read at a 12th grade level. That last best-seller to read at a 12th grade level was Michener's Alaska from the 80s, if I recall correctly.) For those who don't like my reviews, don't read them. I went to bed feeling beaten up. From n0w on I'll respond to no negative comments, I'll in general never read a review of a book that has a rating vastly different than mine, and still, this morning, 7:12 am, I'm still considering the worth of goodreads in general. Publishers quote goodreads on blurbs on their books, and the books are usually ones that do not represent my thoughts. Here's the deal: if 10 people say to me today to shut up and go away, I will do so. I had no idea my reviews and thoughts (4 stars to Harukami's "Killing Commendator") are so upsetting to others. Similarly, I haven't understood that when I say, "I disgree because..." that's a term that is NOT allowed on goodreads. And no matter what, "The Hate U Give" is NOT my favorite book of the past 10 years, it never will be, I'll never agree it is...well, for anything less that $100,000. Then, I'll say its the greatest book ever if you want to start a fund. That favorite 90s mental aberration, "co-dependency" is still hanging around, maybe because President Trump MUST have everyone love his tweets. So, have a nice day. Or don't. It's up to you, no me.<br />I've read and reread this collection, and it has a lot to offer. I still find that my favorite is "Into My Own". I always enjoy a discussion in which no doubt I will eventually say "But the name of that poem is 'The Road Not Taken' and that takes the poem off in a totally different direction." I haven't read a lot of poetry, but have read much Ginsberg ("Howl" is a must for all readers, imho) and Merrill as of late. And so far, I haven't read any poetry collection as good as James Merrill's stunning "A Scattering of Salts." (A five-star rating from me.) This is a relatively new genre from my reading perspective, and I know there is a lot out there just waiting! I already have Merrill's "Late Settings" here at home on my literal "to-read" shelf, but the world is full of unread poetry for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14345213934659630793" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1891888090" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Jocelyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5154274-jocelyn"><img alt="Jocelyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549316010p2/5154274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1891888090?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jocelyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jocelyn" href="/user/show/5154274-jocelyn">Jocelyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Leather Bound 9781435158962" href="/book/show/25668510-robert-frost-selected-poems">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1891888090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12216693387373593038">Robert Frost Selected Poems is a Collection of Robert Frost's work. <br/><br/>Some of my Favorite works include, Home Burial, The Death of the Hired Man, The Line Gang, The Axe Helve, and The Black Cottage. <br/><br/>I enjoyed these five works the most because it shows how people (in my opinion) think and talk to each other. <br/><br/>I think that The Death of the Hired Man is probably my favorite because it goes to show you how some people are stuck in their ways and even when they are offered better, they take the route th</span>
  <span id="freeText12216693387373593038" style="display:none">Robert Frost Selected Poems is a Collection of Robert Frost's work. <br /><br />Some of my Favorite works include, Home Burial, The Death of the Hired Man, The Line Gang, The Axe Helve, and The Black Cottage. <br /><br />I enjoyed these five works the most because it shows how people (in my opinion) think and talk to each other. <br /><br />I think that The Death of the Hired Man is probably my favorite because it goes to show you how some people are stuck in their ways and even when they are offered better, they take the route they know. <br /><br />The Line Gang takes a few reads to grasp. For me, if I don't reread it often, I forget what it means entirely and think of people that are ripping up the forests. I like this poem because it makes you think. <br /><br />The Axe Helve showed that even people that don't know each other that well can care immensely about the other one's safety. In The Axe Helve, the French neighbor that doesn't know the main character very well stops the main character from using his own axe because he (the french neighbor) is afraid for his safety. The French neighbor takes the main character to his house and gives him a better axe for free. <br /><br />The Black Cottage is a reflection of the past that is learned from conversation between two men. The two men talk about the Woman that used to live in the cottage. They talk about the fact that the Woman's children's art is still on the walls. They also say that the children were supposed to visit the house. <br /><br />Overall, I enjoyed this book of poetry. It has nice rhythm and some of the poems have a dark theme. <br /><br />-Jocelyn Kuntz</span>
  <a data-text-id="12216693387373593038" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10345278857435668965"><i>"One of my wishes is that those dark trees,<br/>So old and firm they scarcely show the breeze,<br/>Were not, as it were, the merest mask of gloom,<br/>But stretched away unto the edge of doom.<br/><br/>I do not see why I should ever turn back,<br/>Or those should not set forth upon my track<br/>To overtake me, who should miss me here<br/>And long to know if still I held them dear.<br/><br/>They would not find me changed from him they knew-<br/>Only more sure of all I thought was true."</i><br/><br/>I'll be honest, aside from two or three poems, I was previously n</span>
  <span id="freeText10345278857435668965" style="display:none"><i>"One of my wishes is that those dark trees,<br />So old and firm they scarcely show the breeze,<br />Were not, as it were, the merest mask of gloom,<br />But stretched away unto the edge of doom.<br /><br />I do not see why I should ever turn back,<br />Or those should not set forth upon my track<br />To overtake me, who should miss me here<br />And long to know if still I held them dear.<br /><br />They would not find me changed from him they knew-<br />Only more sure of all I thought was true."</i><br /><br />I'll be honest, aside from two or three poems, I was previously not a huge fan of Robert Frost. My favorite poet happens to be D.H Lawrence, if anyone was wondering. :) Anywho, I saw this book at Barnes &amp; Noble for super cheap and decided to pick it up. I'm actually glad I did, and even more glad that I read it. This was beautifully formatted, and an impressive selection of some of his best works. If you are a fan of Robert Frost or even just a lover of poetry in general, I would highly recommend this book as it is a beautiful rendition of his poems. I find myself appreciating him a lot more now than I did before, which is always a pretty cool outcome. :)</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1190478850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18146664193953254124">Robert Frost used the rhythm of actual speech in a masterful way. The cadence of his writing is very pleasing, almost like music. I did have to read a few of his poems more than once to understand them. It was almost like a puzzle, and if I read it at just the right pace with just the right concentration (not too much and not too little) the meaning would suddenly be unlocked. His poems are truly beautiful in their simplicity. I found that I preferred the ones that didn't have dialogue between t</span>
  <span id="freeText18146664193953254124" style="display:none">Robert Frost used the rhythm of actual speech in a masterful way. The cadence of his writing is very pleasing, almost like music. I did have to read a few of his poems more than once to understand them. It was almost like a puzzle, and if I read it at just the right pace with just the right concentration (not too much and not too little) the meaning would suddenly be unlocked. His poems are truly beautiful in their simplicity. I found that I preferred the ones that didn't have dialogue between two or more people in them, but they were all very absorbing. This was my first entire book of poetry and it won't be the last. It was a very pleasant experience.<br /><br />This fulfilled "a book of poetry" which I listed in place of "a book set in the future" for the 2015 Ultimate Reading Challenge by popsugar.</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781454929123" href="/book/show/35566738-selected-poems-of-robert-frost">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20756278-evelyn?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2740783031" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10606782918117614289">I found this hardcover book at Barnes and Noble. The price was very reasonable. The reason I bought it is for the beautiful woodcut art prints throughout the book. They are black and white or sepia and white and absolutely unbelievable. I've been a fan of Robert Frost since high school, but it was so enjoyable to read many of his poems again with such wonderful artwork to complement it.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('eqnwgWCr6pqBh/IRZbyUGZ1N2AYL0k8lWB6n+sox2jd3aR94q0BphG5N2Sp/BnXWJJleuatCr8eF0WTAlTrtYA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

Among their leaders was Ralph Waldo Emerson, whose essays, lectures, and poems defined the American transcendentalist movement, though he himself disliked the term.\n\nEmerson advocates a rejection of fear-driven conformity, a total independence of thought and spirit, and a life lived in harmony with nature. He believes that Truth lies within each individual, for each is part of a greater whole, a universal “over-soul” through which we transcend the merely mortal.\n\nEmerson was extremely prolific throughout his life; his collected writings fill forty volumes. This edition contains his major works, including Nature, the essays “Self-Reliance,” “The American Scholar,” “The Over-Soul,” “Circles,” “The Poet,” and “Experience,”, and such important poems as “The Rhodora,” “Uriel,” “The Humble-Bee,” “Earth-Song,” “Give All to Love,” and the well-loved “Concord Hymn.”\n\nIncludes a comprehensive glossary of names.\n\nPeter Norberg has been Assistant Professor of English at Saint Joseph’s University in Philadelphia since 1997. Pound\'s lyric genius, his superb technique, and his fresh insight into literary problems make him one of the small company of men who through the centuries have kept poetry alive, and one of the great innovators.\n\nThis book offers a compact yet representative selection of Ezra Pound\'s poems and translations. The span covered is Pound\'s entire writing career, from his early lyrics and the translations of Provençal songs to his English version of Sophocles\' Trachiniae. Included are parts of his best known works—the Chinese translations, the sequence called Hugh Selwyn Mauberly, the Homage to Sextus Propertius. The Cantos, Pound\'s major epic, are presented in generous selections, chosen to emphasize the main themes of the whole poem.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"13721411884625793061\" href=\"#\" sl<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2649664905858975736\" style=\"display:none\">With the publication of his first book of poems, The Weary Blues, in 1926, Langston Hughes electrified readers and launched a renaissance in black writing in America.  The poems Hughes wrote celebrated the experience of invisible men and women: of slaves who &quot;rushed the boots of Washington&quot;; of musicians on Lenox Avenue; of the poor and the lovesick; of losers in &quot;the raffle of night.&quot;  They conveyed that experience in a voice that blended the spoken with the sung, that turned poetic lines into the phrases of jazz and blues, and that ripped through the curtain separating high from popular culture.  They spanned the range from the lyric to the polemic, ringing out &quot;wonder and pain and terror-- and the marrow of the bone of life.&quot;The poems in this collection were chosen by Hughes himself shortly before his death in 1967 and represent work from his entire career, including &quot;The Negro Speaks of Rivers,&quot; &quot;The Weary Blues,&quot; &quot;Still Here,&quot; &quot;Song for a Dark Girl,&quot; &quot;Montage of a Dream Deferred,&quot; and &quot;Refugee in Yeats<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.06 avg rating &mdash; 331 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1899\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer11664379490872411487\">In a letter to his publisher, Yeats referred to The Wind Among the Reeds as \"a book of short lyrics Irish & personal.\" It may also be described as a collection of love poems both intense and indirect. Now considered a watershed in Yeats\'s career, the<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11664379490872411487\" style=\"display:none\">In a letter to his publisher, Yeats referred to The Wind Among the Reeds as &quot;a book of short lyrics Irish &amp; personal.&quot; It may also be described as a collection of love poems both intense and indirect. Now considered a watershed in Yeats\'s career, the book received mixed reviews when it was first published in April of 1899. More recently, Richard Ellmann has asserted that in The Wind Among the Reeds, &quot;Yeats set the method for the modern movement.&quot; For the present volume, Carolyn Holdsworth has assembled and transcribed all holographic materials for each of the 37 poem in the book. She also supplies the complete typescripts and earlier printed versions corrected by Yeats, as well as providing a brief critical introduction. Photographic facsimiles supplement the transcriptions, and the apparatus criticus indicates variant readings. The manuscripts collected here range from drafts on scraps of paper through heavily worked-over typescripts, to neatly copied texts from later years and proof sheets revised by hand. The result is an exhaustive guide to Yeats\'s work on the poems up to the publication of the book and a full record of his post-publication revisions. Offering a close-up view of the various stages of composition of The Wind Among the Reeds, this edition affords a unique understanding of Yeats\'s creative process.<\/span>\n  <a Mais a frente, junta-se a um pequeno grupo de retirantes.\n\nA caminhada é dura, pois a sede, a fome e o choro das crianças que pedem comida são seus companheiros constantes. No percurso, o menino recorda o passado, pensa na vida, no pai, um homem de feições endurecidas pela miséria, herança recebida dos antepassados.\n\nJustino conhece o Chico Cego, violeiro e cantador que tira o seu sustento das esmolas recebidas dos corações caridosos.\n\nOs retirantes chegam a uma vila, e encaminham-se para os albergues em busca de comida, água e tratamento, tudo financiado pelo governo. Mas Chico Cego, Justino e Pitó preferem a feira da vila, nela há a festa da padroeira; há feiras, e trabalho, e Chico anima-se a tocar e cantar suas modinhas. À tardinha, procuram uma ponte para abrigo, alojam-se para o merecido descanso. A amizade entre os três vai crescendo e, logo tornam-se como uma família. Chico Cego não esconde o carinho que sente por Justino e o seu cão.\n\nO menino, por não conhecer dinheiro é enganado e roubado, pondo-se a chorar recebe, então, ajuda de dona Severina, que se sensibiliza com a situação do menino e faz o possível para ajudá-lo. Contrata-o para pequenos serviços, ensina-o a contar, a negociar na feira; em troca o menino recebe: dinheiro, comida e carinho.\n\nDona Severina não se conforma que Justino durma embaixo da ponte, e oferece-lhe moradia. O cego recusa a oferta e parte em busca de um Beato, um homem santo, na ilusão de ficar curado de sua cegueira, deixando Justino com a dona da pensão.\n\nJustino demonstra interesse em aprender a ler, dona Severina o coloca na escola, mas na vila não há ginásio e a senhora, com pretexto de ajudar seu irmão, muda-se com menino para a cidade de São João de Caridé.\n\nO garoto conhece um sociólogo, que o incentiva com os estudos, empresta-lhe livros e lhe orienta sobre a gente sofrida daquela região.\n\nDeslumbrado com as coisas que aprende na escola, Justino quer ser médico para ajudar sua gente; ele traz consigo o conhecimento, mas é necessário aperfeiçoá-lo para que faça melhor. Seus esforços são recompensados, e é escolhido por seus companheiros para ser o orador da sua turma para grande orgulho de dona Severina que chora, emocionada. (...)\n\nPor: Lúcia Helena Gaspareti (http://www.alb.com.br/apoioprof/aprecia/004mott_justino.asp)\n\n<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"8362345582884452354\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));;       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/7715.Robert_Frost">About Robert Frost</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/7715.Robert_Frost"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1605640483p3/7715.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/7715.Robert_Frost">Robert Frost
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10901132240094761206">Flinty, moody, plainspoken and deep, Robert Frost was one of America's most popular 20th-century poets. Frost was farming in Derry, New Hampshire when, at the age of 38, he sold the farm, uprooted his family and moved to England, where he devoted himself to his poetry. His first two books of verse, A Boy's Will (1913) and North of Boston (1914), were immediate successes. In 1915 he returned to the</span>
  <span id="freeText10901132240094761206" style="display:none">Flinty, moody, plainspoken and deep, Robert Frost was one of America's most popular 20th-century poets. Frost was farming in Derry, New Hampshire when, at the age of 38, he sold the farm, uprooted his family and moved to England, where he devoted himself to his poetry. His first two books of verse, A Boy's Will (1913) and North of Boston (1914), were immediate successes. In 1915 he returned to the United States and continued to write while living in New Hampshire and then Vermont. His pastoral images of apple trees and stone fences -- along with his solitary, man-of-few-words poetic voice -- helped define the modern image of rural New England. Frost's poems include "Mending Wall" ("Good fences make good neighbors"), "Stopping by Woods on a Snowy Evening" ("Whose woods these are I think I know"), and perhaps his most famous work, "The Road Not Taken" ("Two roads diverged in a wood, and I-- / I took the one less traveled by"). Frost was awarded the Pulitzer Prize for poetry four times: in 1924, 1931, 1937 and 1943. He also served as "Consultant in Poetry to the Library of Congress" from 1958-59; that position was renamed as Poet Laureate Consultant in Poetry (or simply Poet Laureate) in 1986.<br /><br />Frost recited his poem "The Gift Outright" at the 1961 inauguration of John F. Kennedy... Frost attended both Dartmouth College and Harvard, but did not graduate from either school... Frost preferred traditional rhyme and meter in poetry; his famous dismissal of free verse was, "I'd just as soon play tennis with the net down."<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10901132240094761206" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
